    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

I watched it after watching the netflix series, and I thought it was one of the rare exceptions to the rule where the book is always better. I didn&#39;t think the book was terrible just average. Is The Lottery and We Have Always Lived in the Castle of a similar style and quality or are they superior reads?</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">People who find the book better than the film are people who tend to have read the book first. <br/>I do this a lot with Poirot. I have noticed that if I r<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">People who find the book better than the film are people who tend to have read the book first. <br />I do this a lot with Poirot. I have noticed that if I read the story before watching the TV adaptation is always disappointing. If I watch one before reading I wonder why the story couldn't have been as great as what David Succhet did on the screen.<br />To answer your question read this story. It is short and good.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_2306762598" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2306762598" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2306762598?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=fantasy-horror">fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=philosophy-theology">philosophy-theology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=american">american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2306762598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17563533180170125287"><b>Science Imitating Art</b><br/><br/>Jackson’s story was published in 1948. At the time, and since, it has been praised as insightful and criticised as obscure. But almost 20 years later, the French philosopher, Rene Girard, produced a theory which has a remarkable congruence with its theme and, I think, provides the best explanation of what Jackson was getting at in <i>The Lottery</i>.<br/><br/>Girard argued that our individual desires are never the product of some inner longing but always rather of the imitation of others. We</span>
  <span id="freeText17563533180170125287" style="display:none"><b>Science Imitating Art</b><br /><br />Jackson’s story was published in 1948. At the time, and since, it has been praised as insightful and criticised as obscure. But almost 20 years later, the French philosopher, Rene Girard, produced a theory which has a remarkable congruence with its theme and, I think, provides the best explanation of what Jackson was getting at in <i>The Lottery</i>.<br /><br />Girard argued that our individual desires are never the product of some inner longing but always rather of the imitation of others. We want what other people want. This he called ‘mimetic desire’ and Girard went on to explore the implications of this insight for the next half century.<br /><br />Mimetic desire, according to Girard, has a predictable trajectory that is familiar to advertising executives around the world. One person wants what another has, just because the other has it. This attracts the desire of others in a sort of exponential wave of wanting. <br /><br />But widespread wanting of anything means, first, a shortage of that commodity, and consequently the mutual antagonism of all those who share the same desire. Girard’s contention is that this incipient hostility threatens to create a sort of Hobbesian world, a non-society, in which no cooperative or coordinated action, including effective government, can be established.<br /><br />Human beings, Girard believed, deal with this situation unconsciously and instinctively by the mechanism of ‘scape-goating’, through which a group identifies one of its own members as the cause of its mimetic tension. This individual is both sacred and an object of communal hatred. The elimination of this individual is therefore not just necessary for the welfare of the community, but also forms the basis of religious practice in which the role of the scape-goat is transformed into a noble duty.<br /><br />Girard goes even further in his later work to claim that the ritual establishment of the scape-goat is the most primitive form of representation, and consequently of language, that human beings have demonstrated. In a sense the essential foundation for human power in the world is religious violence which victimizes random members or groups in modern society.<br /><br />Whether or not one agrees with Girard’s anthropology, and there is a substantial body of evidence to recommend it, his literary usefulness is demonstrated by the application of his theory to <i>The Lottery</i>. The theory explains, among other things the liturgical character of the story; its origins in a distant past; its particular relevance to a relatively isolated agricultural community; and its connection to a paternalistic hierarchy whose continued existence depends on the ritual. <br /><br />As far as I am aware, Girard did not read <i>The Lottery</i>; but since he was in America at the time he might have done. In any case, it is certainly remarkable that an author of fiction like Jackson could have written such a tight short story which captures so much of subsequent academic work. Thus demonstrating, if demonstration were needed, the tremendous importance of fiction to cultural life.<br /><br />For an introduction to Girard’s work see: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1818938534?type=review#rating_156135682">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17563533180170125287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2084527193" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2084527193" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2084527193?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2017-completed">xx2017-completed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2084527193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9357610269986474759">This short story is my second classic short story this year and was first published in 1948, yet the story it told is timeless. It is also horrific.<br/><br/>The story begins in a happy, cheerful day late in June (the 27th) which is traditionally the day for the Lottery. This tradition has been going on annually for many years – even the oldest citizen in the town recalls that it had been occurring since before he could remember.<br/><br/>Although some people are talking about other nearby towns that no longer have</span>
  <span id="freeText9357610269986474759" style="display:none">This short story is my second classic short story this year and was first published in 1948, yet the story it told is timeless. It is also horrific.<br /><br />The story begins in a happy, cheerful day late in June (the 27th) which is traditionally the day for the Lottery. This tradition has been going on annually for many years – even the oldest citizen in the town recalls that it had been occurring since before he could remember.<br /><br />Although some people are talking about other nearby towns that no longer have the Lottery, the majority in this village seem to be traditionalists who feel that all kinds of bad things – including crop failures – would occur if they no longer held it.<br /><br />This was all very interesting and then one woman starts to speak out strongly against the process when her husband holds the winning ticket. From there, each family member draws a new ticket and only one of them has a mark on it.<br /><br />From bright to dark to darker and darkest.<br /><br />For me, this story stands out as a caution; of what can happen when people blindly follow along with an idea or concept because it has always been done that way. It may be called duty or responsibility but it can also be called cowardice and going with the flow because it is the path of least resistance. It can also be called ignorance because no-one is giving thought to consequences, nor how they would feel if they were the ultimate “winner”.<br /><br />This is not an enjoyable read, but for sheer power in the writing and the many thoughts and feelings it evokes, it definitely deserves 5 Stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9357610269986474759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3244421676" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3244421676" />
    <a title="Merphy Napier" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41973734-merphy-napier"><img alt="Merphy Napier" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524921716p2/41973734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer3667212834966366725">I really don't have much to say about this. I liked that the majority of this little story was so simple and normal - therefore making the end that much more shocking. I does have a lot of impact - like the children picking out the stones at the beginning and then when he says "let's get it over with quick" and they all rush to the stones, that brought the emotion intended in such a simple story.<br/><br/>But at the same time, what I love about classics like this is the discussion that they invoke. And I </span>
  <span id="freeText3667212834966366725" style="display:none">I really don't have much to say about this. I liked that the majority of this little story was so simple and normal - therefore making the end that much more shocking. I does have a lot of impact - like the children picking out the stones at the beginning and then when he says "let's get it over with quick" and they all rush to the stones, that brought the emotion intended in such a simple story.<br /><br />But at the same time, what I love about classics like this is the discussion that they invoke. And I just don't have a lot to say about this one. I agree that just because a tradition is in place doesn't mean we should mindlessly follow it. But it's kind of a simple discussion so I don't feel compelled to discuss it for very long, which leaves me feeling kinda middle of the road about it. I can see myself bringing this up in a conversation someday if someone is arguing "that's just the way it is" as a reminder that there needs to be sound reasoning behind things and just because we've always done it this way isn't good enough. So I appreciate it for that reason. <br /><br />I liked it, but I just don't have that strong of feelings toward it.</span>

  <span id="freeText3093154913366563085" style="display:none">This seemingly innocuous short story wafted into my consciousness with a halcyon, pastoral scene: an English village on a summer's day, suffused with the scent of blossoming flowers and fresh-cut grass. <div id="review_2084179141" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2084179141" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2084179141?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cecily by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=short-stories-and-novellas">short-stories-and-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2084179141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11498738458791939359">A short story with a nasty sting, that leaves you questioning human nature. I also note now that this is review #666! <br/><br/>Like Ursula Le Guin’s <b>The Ones Who Walked Away from Omelas</b> (which I reviewed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1111738222' rel='nofollow noopener'>HERE</a>), it opens idyllically:<br/>“<i>The morning of June 27th was clear and sunny, with the fresh warmth of a full-summer day; the flowers were blossoming profusely and the grass was richly green. The people of the village began to gather…</i>”, in this case, for the annual public lottery. And like Omelas, there is </span>
  <span id="freeText11498738458791939359" style="display:none">A short story with a nasty sting, that leaves you questioning human nature. I also note now that this is review #666! <br /><br />Like Ursula Le Guin’s <b>The Ones Who Walked Away from Omelas</b> (which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1111738222" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), it opens idyllically:<br />“<i>The morning of June 27th was clear and sunny, with the fresh warmth of a full-summer day; the flowers were blossoming profusely and the grass was richly green. The people of the village began to gather…</i>”, in this case, for the annual public lottery. And like Omelas, there is vague foreshadowing of some darker taboo, universally known, but reluctantly accepted. What little challenge there is, is quickly quashed. The power of crowds, consensus, community, or mob?<br /><br />What made this especially unsettling is that (unlike Omelas) there is no <b>reason</b> given beyond that of tradition. The participants don’t know or remember. And for readers, there are no clues of time (past or future?) or culture (is religion or political regime a factor?) or place. <br /><br />We like to think we’re good people, who would only do cruel things in extremis, when there is no alternative. Jackson’s story suggests the threshold may be much lower if the right/wrong environment is set up. This was published shortly after WW2. Perhaps she was wondering how previously ordinary people came to commit atrocities. <br /><br />See also Kafka's short story, <b>In the Penal Colony</b>, which I reviewed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123462555" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>. Another outsider, like the narrator here, observing strange and disturbing local customs. <br /><br />You can read the story here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://sites.middlebury.edu/individualandthesociety/files/2010/09/jackson_lottery.pdf">https://sites.middlebury.edu/individu...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11498738458791939359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11892254550722602541">What can I possibly even say about this story? <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1449378353ra/17278447.gif' width='400' height='292' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I went on a Twitter deep dive because I am in a reading slump. I decided to Twitter search the reactions to one of the New Yorker's most famous short stories, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303807160' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cat Person.</a> In so doing I found the story that wagged tongues to that extent before. <br/><br/>The reason for that was because, back in 1948, (and I guess the years bracketing that) The New Yorker published stories without showing whether they were fact or fiction. And my, isn't that an effective way t</span>
  <span id="freeText11892254550722602541" style="display:none">What can I possibly even say about this story? <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1449378353ra/17278447.gif" width="400" height="292" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I went on a Twitter deep dive because I am in a reading slump. I decided to Twitter search the reactions to one of the New Yorker's most famous short stories, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303807160" rel="nofollow noopener">Cat Person.</a> In so doing I found the story that wagged tongues to that extent before. <br><br>The reason for that was because, back in 1948, (and I guess the years bracketing that) The New Yorker published stories without showing whether they were fact or fiction. And my, isn't that an effective way to rouse the indignation of the middle class. <br><br>Before Cat Person, this was the story that created the most hubbub and according to thoughtco.com, <i> It has been adapted for radio, theater, television, and even ballet. The Simpsons television show included a reference to the story in its "Dog of Death" episode (season three).</i><br><br>The Lottery is a story set in a small town in America. If that isn't enough to set your heart to dread... have you never seen any Hollywood horror movie? Everything happens in those small towns. <br><br>Jackson herself said, <i>"[I wrote this] to shock the story's readers with a graphic demonstration of the pointless violence and general inhumanity in their own lives."</i> <br><br>Well then Ms Jackson, mission accomplished. <br><br><a href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/1948/06/26/the-lottery" rel="nofollow noopener">You can read it here. </a><br><br>PPS// The day this story is set, June 27, is my born day. I'm rightfully spooked af.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13647891126482661772">How do you rate something that keeps you from sleeping?<br/><br/>I know that I thought it was brilliantly done; Jackson set the tone so well.   <span id="freeText13647891126482661772" style="display:none">How do you rate something that keeps you from sleeping?<br /><br />I know that I thought it was brilliantly done; Jackson set the tone so well. She paints a bright, cheerful picture to start. It's a beautiful sunny day and the whole town is gathering, like for a town picnic. They're drawing for something, you think, I wonder what that is. <br /><br />It's not until the 5th last paragraph that Jackson pulls the rug out from under your feet - and so quickly that I had to re-read the pivotal line about three times before I realized what was happening. When I did I was horrified.<br /><br />It made me uncomfortable, gave me a terrible feeling in the pit of my stomach, and I didn't sleep well for days afterward. It was powerful, it was terrifying, but I'm not sure whether I liked it or not.<br /><br />(And I can't believe they have kids read this story in high school).</span>

  <b>
    <i>"People ain’t the way they used to be."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote>I've seen this short story a lot of times before and don't know why couldn't pick this up. It's full of tension! I haven't read a story having that much tension. There was no specific world and character building but still it managed to keep me interested. <br/><br/>There's a lottery happens on annual basis. And the entire village has to take part in it. And it's a whole different kind of lottery than we see nowadays. That's all I can tell you without spo</span>
  <span id="freeText8920132470236673173" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"People ain’t the way they used to be."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote>I've seen this short story a lot of times before and don't know why couldn't pick this up. It's full of tension! I haven't read a story having that much tension. There was no specific world and character building but still it managed to keep me interested. <br /><br />There's a lottery happens on annual basis. And the entire village has to take part in it. And it's a whole different kind of lottery than we see nowadays. That's all I can tell you without spoiling. (<i>Does it feel like I've spoilt already?</i>)<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ While reading, I was keep wondering what the winner is gonna win. But turns out he/she will get a rain of stones! That was quite disturbing. And this fact wasn't revealed till the last half page. :( <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I can't say it's a good story. But I can say it will keep your attention till the end. So for that, I can recommend this story. <br /><br />3 February, 2019<br /><br /><br /><b>PS:</b> <i> I was supposed to finish Ruin and Rising. But unfortunately got ill. Today I got better. So thought to read this story as a warm-up. :)<br />Now, I'm gonna finish the Grisha series. ^^</i></span>

<div id="review_2656663886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2656663886" />
    <a title="Dem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2752994-dem"><img alt="Dem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552868566p2/2752994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2656663886?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dem" href="/user/show/2752994-dem">Dem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2752994-dem?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2656663886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10648989091701750923"><b> 3.5 Stars<br/>What a quirky and strange short story describing a fictional small town which observes an annual ritual known as "the lottery". Less is certainly more in the case of this short story as its one of those books that certainly makes you think outside the box and packs a punch in its delivery.</b> <br/><br/>I like a <u> little </u> dystopian every now and then and this one I found quite strange and eerie and yet its message in many ways is played out in modern society every day. The power of this story for me </span>
  <span id="freeText10648989091701750923" style="display:none"><b> 3.5 Stars<br />What a quirky and strange short story describing a fictional small town which observes an annual ritual known as "the lottery". Less is certainly more in the case of this short story as its one of those books that certainly makes you think outside the box and packs a punch in its delivery.</b> <br /><br />I like a <u> little </u> dystopian every now and then and this one I found quite strange and eerie and yet its message in many ways is played out in modern society every day. The power of this story for me was more in what was left unsaid or un explained and I enjoyed trying to fill in the blanks.<br /> The setting of the story really shows that that violence and evil can take place anywhere and in any context and I can understand why the author choose to write this story in 1948.<br /><br />I am not a fan of short stories in general but The Lottery really did peak my interest and was quite thought provoking.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10648989091701750923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11749456590371155095"><br/>After reading my first novel by Shirley Jackson ("We Have Always Lived In The Castle"), I came across references to a 'famous' short story that started a major hubbub in the newspaper that first published it. Unfortunately, I also came across spoilers for what the story is about, so it's impact was somewhow lessened.<br/>Thus, I will not review it here, hoping some other reader might still come with a fresh mind to it.<br/><br/>I will only mention it is worth reading, it shows the author's distinctive touch of</span>
  <span id="freeText11749456590371155095" style="display:none"><br />After reading my first novel by Shirley Jackson ("We Have Always Lived In The Castle"), I came across references to a 'famous' short story that started a major hubbub in the newspaper that first published it. Unfortunately, I also came across spoilers for what the story is about, so it's impact was somewhow lessened.<br />Thus, I will not review it here, hoping some other reader might still come with a fresh mind to it.<br /><br />I will only mention it is worth reading, it shows the author's distinctive touch of evil in the most ordinary places, and reveals unpleasant truths about our mob mentality and our readiness to embrace dangerous ideas. Considering the story was written shortly after World War Two, its relevance and the accuracy of Jackson's insights have not been exaggerated. <br /><br />It takes a great writer to say so much with only a few words.</span>

  <span id="freeText5592757347326578385" style="display:none">If lotteries are supposed to be so fair, why don't they ever feel that way?<br /><br />I just re-read this story as it is the first one in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8966218.Brave_New_Worlds" title="Brave New Worlds by John Joseph Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Brave New Worlds</a> collection. I gave it an extra star as a result. Knowing exactly what's going to happen gives reading this an additional dimension of eeriness, so I'd definitely recommend reading and coming back to this one at a later date. Not only because of its major influence on later dystopias, but also because of the way it draws you in. In the course of just ten pages it managed to make me feel part of a village, member of a community in which I know every face and what horrors they hide. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The "twist" wasn't extremely surprising and maybe was less of a twist rather than a logical culmination of a situation that is so obviously foreboding, but together with the build-up and setting it does leave a distinct impression. An interesting idea, used and built upon by at least one contemporary YA author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1458427406?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">I happen to have read recently</a>, that made sure that <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> this little story is a quick and pleasurable read, packing an enormous punch!</span>

  <span id="freeText3222586957962138785" style="display:none">I should thank my high school lit teacher for making us read this story and scaring the shit out of us back then. I still read this from time to time and I've recommended it to a bunch of friends and it still manages to creep the hell out of me. <br /><br />And while there had been many other stories with similar premise (sort of) since then, The Lottery still stands as one of the yardstick in this genre. Just don't read this prior to going to bed because you might not be able to sleep at all. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer993154115334533320">I read this short story again recently and was struck, as ever, by Jackson's mastery. It's only about 10 pages long, and every word is perfect. It would make my list of the best short stories ever written.<br/><br/>"The Lottery" opens in a village in late June, and the 300 citizens are assembling in the town square. Each family stands together and the head of the household must draw a piece of paper from a black box. We learn that the lottery has something to do with a good harvest, but the true meaning o</span>
  <span id="freeText993154115334533320" style="display:none">I read this short story again recently and was struck, as ever, by Jackson's mastery. It's only about 10 pages long, and every word is perfect. It would make my list of the best short stories ever written.<br /><br />"The Lottery" opens in a village in late June, and the 300 citizens are assembling in the town square. Each family stands together and the head of the household must draw a piece of paper from a black box. We learn that the lottery has something to do with a good harvest, but the true meaning of the paper isn't revealed until the last page -- and woe to anyone who would spoil it for a reader who has not had the experience of seeing it unfold for themselves.<br /><br />The story was first published in 1948, and is considered to be an American classic. I highly recommend it.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1714528798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1224501984797787130">Shirley Jackson's classic short story The Lottery is perhaps the basis for The Hunger Games, which is hardly a favorite of mine. Jackson use of prose has me at the edge of my seat and has be eagerly awaiting the ending. The use of language merits a 5 but for me the story is grotesque so the whole story earns a 3. I can see here, however, why Jackson is highly regarded as an author, but her stories are most definitely not my taste. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12040955560883907154">I found out about this book from <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9_DwfmsxlWo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Annamaria's book video</a>. She gave no spoilers away, but I thought I knew what <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6219656.The_Lottery' title='The Lottery by Shirley Jackson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Lottery</a> was going to be about and I wanted to read it.<br/><br/>If you read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92625.The_Ones_Who_Walk_Away_from_Omelas' title='The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas by Ursula K. Le Guin' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas</a>, you, too, know what this book is about, although <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6219656.The_Lottery' title='The Lottery by Shirley Jackson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Lottery</a> was published a few years before Ursula K. Le Guin's book.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454852719ra/18022297.gif' width='500' height='264' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>That is one question you <b>
  <i>
    <u>do not</u>
  </i>
</b> want this book to give you an answer to. <br/><br/>There is one town where there's an annual lottery and <b>all</b> the people have to tak</span>
  <span id="freeText12040955560883907154" style="display:none">I found out about this book from <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9_DwfmsxlWo" rel="nofollow noopener">Annamaria's book video</a>. She gave no spoilers away, but I thought I knew what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6219656.The_Lottery" title="The Lottery by Shirley Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lottery</a> was going to be about and I wanted to read it.<br><br>If you read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92625.The_Ones_Who_Walk_Away_from_Omelas" title="The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas</a>, you, too, know what this book is about, although <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6219656.The_Lottery" title="The Lottery by Shirley Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lottery</a> was published a few years before Ursula K. Le Guin's book.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1454852719ra/18022297.gif" width="500" height="264" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>That is one question you <b>
  <i>
    <u>do not</u>
  </i>
</b> want this book to give you an answer to. <br><br>There is one town where there's an annual lottery and <b>all</b> the people have to take part (no matter their age). The story being so short, it's hard to talk about it without spoiling it. What happens to the winner is disgusting, troubling, nerve-wreaking... you may add any other adjective you feel like. And the horror I felt is increased by the fact that there are some parts of the world where such a thing happens <b>now-a-days</b>. Not because of a lottery of course, because of other reasons. And it's still barbaric.<br><br>The characters in the story are unimportant. They could be anybody. And in spite of knowing that other towns have renounced the lottery, they go on with it. I was going to ask how would anybody let tradition transform them into a monster, but I remembered that there are still such monsters among us.<br><br>Having read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92625.The_Ones_Who_Walk_Away_from_Omelas" title="The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas by Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Ones Who Walk Away from Omelas</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10452275.Ponies" title="Ponies by Kij Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Ponies</a>, now I am accustomed (it's horrible I'm using this word) to stories like these, and I can give <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6219656.The_Lottery" title="The Lottery by Shirley Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lottery</a> more than 2 or 3 stars and not place it on my <i>least favourite shelf</i>.<br><br>You can listen to this story <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YmPmdJO-zYs&amp;index=5&amp;list=PLcYWzIBUausy7xxWTyMM7lqzSDyXGiZJ7" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</span>

<div id="review_1416144202" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1416144202" />
    <a title="Rachel Reads Ravenously" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14514998-rachel-reads-ravenously"><img alt="Rachel Reads Ravenously" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539149211p2/14514998.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1416144202?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Reads Ravenously" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Reads Ravenously" href="/user/show/14514998-rachel-reads-ravenously">Rachel Reads Ravenously</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Rachel Reads Ravenously by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/11757728-geri-reads">Geri Reads</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1416144202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12222342293874493505">Well that was a bit of a mindfuck! I asked on Facebook for horror recommendations and Geri rec'd me this one. Geri, I'll be sending you the bill for my new therapy sessions after this! Jkjk.<br/><br/>The Lottery starts out innocently, in fact if I hadn't known it was a horror/spooky story I never would have suspected it would go where it did. Considering this is only a few pages it's one of the best written short stories I've ever read. I have got to read more by this author.<br/><br/>Are you intrigued? I DARE you </span>
  <span id="freeText12222342293874493505" style="display:none">Well that was a bit of a mindfuck! I asked on Facebook for horror recommendations and Geri rec'd me this one. Geri, I'll be sending you the bill for my new therapy sessions after this! Jkjk.<br /><br />The Lottery starts out innocently, in fact if I hadn't known it was a horror/spooky story I never would have suspected it would go where it did. Considering this is only a few pages it's one of the best written short stories I've ever read. I have got to read more by this author.<br /><br />Are you intrigued? I DARE you to read this. <span id="freeTextContainer1402237693300034715">Hmm. Well. <br/><br/>*sigh* <br/><br/>Shirley Jackson and I have this <i>thing</i>. I want to like her stories, and I get all "Yay! I'm going to just LOVE this one because THIS is the story that people think of when they think of Shirley Jackson!"... except, that's kind of been all of them, and they all have let me down in some way. <br/><br/>This one... well... I think it needed more violence. The climax was just kind of "...andthenthishappenedtheend." It needed more oomph. More, "Holy shit are you kidding me? WTF!" <br/><br/>Oh yes, yes, I</span>
  <span id="freeText1402237693300034715" style="display:none">Hmm. Well. <br /><br />*sigh* <br /><br />Shirley Jackson and I have this <i>thing</i>. I want to like her stories, and I get all "Yay! I'm going to just LOVE this one because THIS is the story that people think of when they think of Shirley Jackson!"... except, that's kind of been all of them, and they all have let me down in some way. <br /><br />This one... well... I think it needed more violence. The climax was just kind of "...andthenthishappenedtheend." It needed more oomph. More, "Holy shit are you kidding me? WTF!" <br /><br />Oh yes, yes, I know. Shirley Jackson is the Master, excuse me, Mistress of the Psychological Whammy. I bow down before her genius and revoke my right to opinion and criticism. <br /><br />Pfft. *eyeroll* She may be called the Mistress of the Psychological Whammy, but I've yet to actually GET that from her stories. To me, it always feels like... I dunno, she just gets off with writing a half-assed story with a kicker in the last line, and then we're all supposed to be awed by the immensity of the talent it took to do it. <br /><br />I'm no writer, so this isn't an "I can do it better!" tirade. I just don't get why Shirley Jackson's stories are so revered. Maybe I'm just doing it wrong and not analyzing them enough. Maybe that's it. <br /><br />Sure. I'll save you the work (spoiler alert): <br /><br />Any tradition that leads up to murdering your neighbors is bad, mmmkay? </span>

<div id="review_1912527314" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1912527314" />
    <a title="Mohsin Maqbool" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3332742-mohsin-maqbool"><img alt="Mohsin Maqbool" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373637583p2/3332742.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1912527314?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mohsin Maqbool" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mohsin Maqbool" href="/user/show/3332742-mohsin-maqbool">Mohsin Maqbool</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781583415849" href="/book/show/6219655-the-lottery">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1912527314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15818844508702493036"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487028556i/21970271.jpg' width='400' height='400' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>"The morning of June 27th was clear and sunny, with the fresh warmth of a full-summer day; the flowers were blossoming profusely and the grass was richly green." But how can the faces of the villagers be so forlorn and grim on such a beautiful day!?</b><br/><br/>I HAD come to know towards the fag end of last year only that Shirley Jackson writes tales of terror. She is supposed to build the suspense slowly and steadily, often taking you by surprise towards the end. So when I started reading "The Lottery" toda</span>
  <span id="freeText15818844508702493036" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487028556i/21970271.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>"The morning of June 27th was clear and sunny, with the fresh warmth of a full-summer day; the flowers were blossoming profusely and the grass was richly green." But how can the faces of the villagers be so forlorn and grim on such a beautiful day!?</b><br><br>I HAD come to know towards the fag end of last year only that Shirley Jackson writes tales of terror. She is supposed to build the suspense slowly and steadily, often taking you by surprise towards the end. So when I started reading "The Lottery" today, I was glad that she wrote uplifting stories too.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487028556i/21970272.jpg" width="220" height="341" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Luck of the draw: Shirley Jackson</b><br><br> There is this village where a lottery is held each year. It has a population of 700 villagers each of whom participates in it. A black box is kept on a three-legged stool in the town square. Each villager's name is called. All s/he has to do is pick out a piece of paper and keep it folded in her/his hand without looking at it.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487028556i/21970273.png" width="420" height="294" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>I hope you win soon.</b><br> <br> While reading the story I kept wondering what the prize would be and who the lucky winner would be. Maybe a large amount of cash or a piece of furniture or a horse-driven wagon for the woman with the largest number of children. Or maybe a big fat cow for the oldest man. <br> Only Mr Dunbar could not make it to the lottery because of having a broken leg. But it does not make much of a difference as a family member can substitute for him. <br> The last to arrive before drawing starts is Mrs Hutchinson. There is no way she was going to miss The Lottery, as it came only once in 365 days on June 27th.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380368903i/721496.png" width="431" height="444" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>The odd one out gets The Prize.</b> <br><br> After having read two-thirds of the story it suddenly dawned upon me that this is a different kind of lottery, one that is dark and sinister. The villagers are certainly not as innocent and as jovial as they seem. I cringed my face in horror as soon as I realised what was going to happen towards the end.<br> Shirley Jackson had written the story in 1948 in the aftermath of the Second World War. Does it allude that one human being can be thirsty for the blood of another just as they are during wars or the way gladiators were in a Roman arena, cheered on by the excited onlookers?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413144468i/11471127.jpg" width="259" height="194" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Jackson received hate mail. People wrote to ask if the short story were fiction—and if not, where did these outlandish rituals take place, anyway?</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15818844508702493036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CSMdTZ0VptOY3GTNNJxqYii1Tq0MYqjGqjxUBfzQaG7Ez+vUSB1AnTMJhO61yVHmRZXAyaJnh4zlUzCedYgy4Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

It is a<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15462149653411451167\" style=\"display:none\">A Good Man Is Hard to Find is Flannery O\'Connor\'s most famous and most discussed story. O\'Connor herself singled it out by making it the title piece of her first collection and the story she most often chose for readings or talks to students. It is an unforgettable tale, both riveting and comic, of the confrontation of a family with violence and sudden death. More than anything else O\'Connor ever wrote, this story mixes the comedy, violence, and religious concerns that characterize her fiction.\n\nThis casebook for the story includes an introduction by the editor, a chronology of the author\'s life, the authoritative text of the story itself, comments and letters by O\'Connor about the story, critical essays, and a bibliography. The critical essays span more than twenty years of commentary and suggest several approaches to the story--formalistic, thematic, deconstructionist-- all within the grasp of the undergraduate, while the introduction also points interested students toward still other resources. It was adapted for an episode of Alfred Hitchcock Presents and starred Barbara Bel Geddes. Originally broadcast on April 13, 1958, it was one of only 17 AHP episodes directed by Hitchcock himself.           $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_92625').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_92625').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_92625').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13388.Shirley_Jackson">About Shirley Jackson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/13388.Shirley_Jackson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1550251468p3/13388.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5579910795710404681">Shirley Jackson was an influential American author. A popular writer in her time, her work has received increasing attention from literary critics in recent years. She has influenced such writers as Stephen King, Nigel Kneale, and Richard Matheson.<br/><br/>She is best known for her dystopian short story, "The Lottery" (1948), which suggests there is a deeply unsettling underside to bucolic, smalltown Ameri</span>
  <span id="freeText5579910795710404681" style="display:none">Shirley Jackson was an influential American author. A popular writer in her time, her work has received increasing attention from literary critics in recent years. She has influenced such writers as Stephen King, Nigel Kneale, and Richard Matheson.<br /><br />She is best known for her dystopian short story, "The Lottery" (1948), which suggests there is a deeply unsettling underside to bucolic, smalltown America. In her critical biography of Shirley Jackson, Lenemaja Friedman notes that when Shirley Jackson's story "The Lottery" was published in the June 28, 1948, issue of <i>The New Yorker</i>, it received a response that "no <i>New Yorker</i> story had ever received." Hundreds of letters poured in that were characterized by, as Jackson put it, "bewilderment, speculation and old-fashioned abuse."<br /><br />Jackson's husband, the literary critic Stanley Edgar Hyman, wrote in his preface to a posthumous anthology of her work that "she consistently refused to be interviewed, to explain or promote her work in any fashion, or to take public stands and be the pundit of the Sunday supplements. She believed that her books would speak for her clearly enough over the years." Hyman insisted the darker aspects of Jackson's works were not, as some critics claimed, the product of "personal, even neurotic, fantasies", but that Jackson intended, as "a sensitive and faithful anatomy of our times, fitting symbols for our distressing world of the concentration camp and the Bomb", to mirror humanity's Cold War-era fears. Jackson may even have taken pleasure in the subversive impact of her work, as revealed by Hyman's statement that she "was always proud that the Union of South Africa banned <i>The Lottery</i>', and she felt that they at least understood the story".<br /><br />In 1965, Jackson died of heart failure in her sleep, at her home in North Bennington Vermont, at the age of 48. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5579910795710404681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
